<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_1802c0f5-e513-4b7b-841a-d3e4edae09db'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

</script>

<span id="freeTextContainer9179816102750095365">In the vein of <em>Jurassic Park</em>, this high-concept thriller follows a group of graduate students lured to Hawaii to work for a mysterious biotech company—only to find themselves cast out into the rain forest, with nothing but their scientific expertise and wits to protect them. An instant classic, <em>Micro</em> pits nature against technology in vintage Crichton fashion. Completed by </span>
  <span id="freeText9179816102750095365" style="display:none">In the vein of <em>Jurassic Park</em>, this high-concept thriller follows a group of graduate students lured to Hawaii to work for a mysterious biotech company—only to find themselves cast out into the rain forest, with nothing but their scientific expertise and wits to protect them. An instant classic, <em>Micro</em> pits nature against technology in vintage Crichton fashion. Completed by visionary science writer Richard Preston, this boundary-pushing thriller melds scientific fact with pulse-pounding fiction to create yet another masterpiece of sophisticated, cutting-edge entertainment.</span>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/76221183-ethan-green"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_100x100-259587f1619f5253426a4fa6fb508831.png" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/76221183-ethan-green">Ethan Green</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Let me offer an alternative view. I read the forward, something I almost never do, and it mentioned the author's inspiration to write this book was du<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Let me offer an alternative view. I read the forward, something I almost never do, and it mentioned the author's inspiration to write this book was due to his dislike that many people are no longer taught about the natural world and how he was disappointed they couldn't name common plants / animals. <br /><br />So my theory is he wanted to show off something the average reader would go "cool!" about. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_231828521" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/231828521" />
    <a title="Mandy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5772626-mandy"><img alt="Mandy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1350335276p2/5772626.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/231828521?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mandy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mandy" href="/user/show/5772626-mandy">Mandy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer231828521" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4721601169222683562">What a disappointment. I stopped halfway— at the time I really couldn't continue; I've never been irritated by a book before but I did rally round and read to the end of the thing. I'm assuming my irritation was in part because of my expectations—‘an unfinished Crichton novel!’ I thought—what fun! But this isn't Crichton: whoever Mr Preston is, he's a cheap impostor whose prose employs words like 'goop', 'gunk' and 'goo'. Crichton didn't.<br/><br/>But then there's the technology. Crichton's time travel ha</span>
  <span id="freeText4721601169222683562" style="display:none">What a disappointment. I stopped halfway— at the time I really couldn't continue; I've never been irritated by a book before but I did rally round and read to the end of the thing. I'm assuming my irritation was in part because of my expectations—‘an unfinished Crichton novel!’ I thought—what fun! But this isn't Crichton: whoever Mr Preston is, he's a cheap impostor whose prose employs words like 'goop', 'gunk' and 'goo'. Crichton didn't.<br /><br />But then there's the technology. Crichton's time travel had consequences on a quantum level, and made sense in its own reality-distorted sense. Timeline may have been a mainly historical techno-romp, but at least it had internal consistency. Now lets look at the technology of Micro, ignoring the massively stupid boss character. How can the protagonists shrunken anatomy breathe or digest the oversized molecules they take in—I can't help but feel that the real Michael Crichton would have addressed the fact that the cells in the annoying students' bodies would be a different size to real-world chemicals, or maybe even abandoned the plot as a dead end—one to be stowed away in the office drawer as a failure until... oh, he's dead—wait, look: there's a manuscript marked 'Not to be published ever ever ever' let's publish this... we'll get some guy to finish it.<br /><br />Back to the plot. As for their exploits—this is no H. G. Wells romp or Conan Doyle jollity; Crichton insisted on factual plausibility in his novels, and in this one there is little plausibility and an absolute mass of expedited expedition extrapolation. Most of it entirely extraordinary and yet necessary to the rather obvious plot. The initial concept is great, and I'm assuming that Crichton's influence got as far as page 30 before it vanished. I'm sorry—the majority of the book is an ill-conceived implausible pratfall of a novel.<br /><br />For light relief, try adding the adverb 'magically' in front of anything the stranded pillocks manage to do which is vaguely implausible (i.e. almost anything): '...which Peter peeled out of the center of dead grass stems which had magically stayed dry despite the rain', 'Rick magically lit the fire with the windproof lighter' (always take one if you're going to be unexpectedly miniaturised), 'Wait a minute, guys, I've magically found something!' (But will it be useful? Well... yes, actually. Surprise!), 'The mosquito magically thundered off, wobbling in the air.'<br /><br />Plotting be damned - anything can happen with the right combination of the Crichton name and the appropriate gods to call forth from the (nano) machine...</span>
  <a data-text-id="4721601169222683562" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_239890924" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/239890924" />
    <a title="Ryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1500718-ryan"><img alt="Ryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1238846167p2/1500718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/239890924?book_show_action=true">Nov 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan" href="/user/show/1500718-ryan">Ryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer239890924" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16677243751505603537">Do you believe in redemption?<br/><br/>We often abandon authors once they disappoint us, and Michael Crichton never really found his way again after 1990's <i>Jurassic Park</i>. In fact, in his later works Crichton seemed to spiral out of control, so I'd more or less given up on him. And I don't think I was the only one. Thankfully, Crichton's new book <i>Micro</i> could easily be adapted for film, suggesting that he had returned to form before he passed away in 2008.<br/><br/>There will be a temptation to compare <i>Micro</i> to <i>Prey</i>,</span>
  <span id="freeText16677243751505603537" style="display:none">Do you believe in redemption?<br /><br />We often abandon authors once they disappoint us, and Michael Crichton never really found his way again after 1990's <i>Jurassic Park</i>. In fact, in his later works Crichton seemed to spiral out of control, so I'd more or less given up on him. And I don't think I was the only one. Thankfully, Crichton's new book <i>Micro</i> could easily be adapted for film, suggesting that he had returned to form before he passed away in 2008.<br /><br />There will be a temptation to compare <i>Micro</i> to <i>Prey</i>, Crichton's novel about the dangers of nanotechnology swarms with artificial intelligence. Here, it is the people, as opposed to the machines, that are shrunk down to miniature size -- thanks to the power of magnets. However, we might get further comparing it to <i>Jurassic Park</i>. Once they are in the "micro world," our scientists are forced to traverse a rainforest that is crawling with gigantic predators: insects.<br /><br />The writing is often atrocious and it's easier to forget that characterization and motivation exist than it is to expect either, particularly with our villains. Still, I found it helpful to remember that <i>Micro</i> was an unfinished novel (completed by Richard Preston). I also reminded myself that we would eventually reach that ironic moment in Crichton's techno thrillers where scientists battle the creatures they would normally study in labs.<br /><br />It would seem that the retreat of scientists to the laboratory is the general inspiration for the novel. In his introduction, Crichton criticizes the scientific community for failing to do field work and for relying on computer models. Crichton suggests that it is difficult to capture the awesome complexity of nature on a computer screen. So, read a book about scientists actually getting their hands dirty as they wrestle with mites, the intelligence of ants, and the chemical weapons of beetles. Actually, anyone that's afraid of insects will be more likely to retreat to the lab than to hike through the rainforest after reading <i>Micro</i>.<br /><br />Regardless of his aims, Crichton's narrative premise is solid. The insects are often quite spooky, the micro-sized tools that the scientists come up with to survive in the micro world are pretty cool, and the pace is fast. In other words, he's covered all the bases.<br /><br />Still, <i>Micro</i> is not as good as Crichton's greatest techno thrillers: <i>Congo</i>, <i>Sphere</i>, and <i>Jurassic Park</i>. But having finished <i>Micro</i>, I like to think that Crichton ended his career on a high note.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16677243751505603537" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_433429862" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/433429862" />
    <a title="Marvin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2160536-marvin"><img alt="Marvin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485672728p2/2160536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/433429862?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marvin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marvin" href="/user/show/2160536-marvin">Marvin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2160536-marvin?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer433429862" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12667121874657971877">There should be a law against authors finishing up other authors' books. It never ends well.<br/><br/>But I do not think Richard Preston did all that much damage to the novel. In fact, from the unfinished introduction by Michael Crichton at the beginning of the book, Crichton appears to be in his preachy mode as he was in his most annoying books like his Japanophobic <i>Rising Sun</i> and his rant against the idea of global warming titled <i>State of Fear</i>. The fact that Crichton was already writing an intro may be </span>
  <span id="freeText12667121874657971877" style="display:none">There should be a law against authors finishing up other authors' books. It never ends well.<br /><br />But I do not think Richard Preston did all that much damage to the novel. In fact, from the unfinished introduction by Michael Crichton at the beginning of the book, Crichton appears to be in his preachy mode as he was in his most annoying books like his Japanophobic <i>Rising Sun</i> and his rant against the idea of global warming titled <i>State of Fear</i>. The fact that Crichton was already writing an intro may be a hint that most of the book was finished anyways. If this was the case, Preston managed to erase any of Crichton usually annoying rants and toned it down to a straight-forward techno-thriller.<br /><br />The problem is that it isn't a very good techno-thriller to begin with. To be honest, I think <i>Prey</i> was the last good novel Crichton wrote and <i>Micro</i> did not convince me that the author was due for any kind of revival of creativity before his untimely death. In this unfinished novel, it is impossible to tell where Crichton starts and where he left us. This is probably a nod of admiration in how well Preston merges their styles. Yet <i>Micro</i> never steps off on its own to begin with. One-dimensional evil genius grapples with under-rated if whiny graduate students is as far as any character development goes. As for the plot, it is equal parts, Asimov's <i>Fantastic Voyage</i>, <i>Dr Cyclops</i>, and <i>Honey, I Shrunk the Kids</i>, all being better if not as technically knowledgeable as Crichton and Preston. <i>Micro</i> certainly will not sully the reputation of Michael Crichton but it doesn't bode well for a last bow either.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12667121874657971877" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_896242892" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/896242892" />
    <a title="Will M." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20408288-will-m"><img alt="Will M." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452951660p2/20408288.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/896242892?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will M." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will M." href="/user/show/20408288-will-m">Will M.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20408288-will-m?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20408288-will-m?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20408288-will-m?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20408288-will-m?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer896242892" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4671974471123260634">I read this about a month ago, and only now have I realized that this needs a better review from me. For a Crichton (or half a Crichton) novel, this really has a low rating. At first I couldn't understand why, because this really has to be one of my favorite novels.<br/><br/>After reading a lot of reviews, I realized that people were rating this book based on their past experience with Crichton's novels. I will be honest and say that this was my first novel of Crichton, so maybe that's the reason for my l</span>
  <span id="freeText4671974471123260634" style="display:none">I read this about a month ago, and only now have I realized that this needs a better review from me. For a Crichton (or half a Crichton) novel, this really has a low rating. At first I couldn't understand why, because this really has to be one of my favorite novels.<br /><br />After reading a lot of reviews, I realized that people were rating this book based on their past experience with Crichton's novels. I will be honest and say that this was my first novel of Crichton, so maybe that's the reason for my liking of it. <br /><br />The problem with unfinished novels is that people tend to judge it even before opening the novel. This is not the first series I've encountered that people bash the book right away just because they've read the author's past novels and they criticize the writing of the one who finished the novel, or in some cases, helped writing (James Patterson). <br /><br />Before reading this book all I knew about Michael Crichton was that he wrote Jurassic Park, and I've only seen the movie then. I haven't actually read the book so like I said, I wasn't expecting much from the novel. I didn't buy it because of the author, I bought it because the plot seemed interesting to me. <br /><br />I'm aware that I'm not a hardcore Sci-Fi fan, but I'm starting to become one. I have to admit that Sci-Fi has to be one of my favorite genres now. For me this book contains a lot of realistic Science Fiction. Realistic in a way that if a person were to shrink, the problems faced by the characters in the book were very believable, and that for me was the reason why I loved this book in the first place. I felt like I was one of the characters, and their experiences during their "shrunk adventure" really caught my interest. In fact, I even ordered some more books about people shrinking (Sci-Fi of course).<br /><br />All I can say is that if you are to read this book, and you've read a lot of Michael Crichton books, I recommend that you try and forget first some of Crichton's work and have an open-mind for this one. I'm sure its not going to be the best for you guys, it might not even make it to your favorites shelf, but I'm sure that one way or another you'd find this novel as a good read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4671974471123260634" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_277405996" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/277405996" />
    <a title="Nadia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7057785-nadia"><img alt="Nadia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345718038p2/7057785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/277405996?book_show_action=true">Feb 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nadia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nadia" href="/user/show/7057785-nadia">Nadia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7057785-nadia?shelf=recommends">recommends</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer277405996" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6051736906024296913">"Honey, I Shrunk the Kids" meets "And Then There Were None" in this lame attempt at pseudo-science fiction. Now, I'm all for the suspension of disbelief, but a magnetic field that shrinks people and machines down to half an inch, in seconds, without any kind of structural damage? Please. Dinosaurs, time machines, nano bots, no problem. But a shrink ray is going to take a bit more convincing. Shall I add that the idea of having seven grad students in the same lab, all of whom happen to be working</span>
  <span id="freeText6051736906024296913" style="display:none">"Honey, I Shrunk the Kids" meets "And Then There Were None" in this lame attempt at pseudo-science fiction. Now, I'm all for the suspension of disbelief, but a magnetic field that shrinks people and machines down to half an inch, in seconds, without any kind of structural damage? Please. Dinosaurs, time machines, nano bots, no problem. But a shrink ray is going to take a bit more convincing. Shall I add that the idea of having seven grad students in the same lab, all of whom happen to be working on completely unrelated projects that mysteriously are perfectly adapted to a world of insects is implausible? Nah. Setting aside the bad science and even the murderous corporate psychopath as the villain, I still have to say that the characters were flat and uninteresting, the plot was predictable and dull, the death scenes were high in gore and low in suspense and lacked the scientific attention to detail that usually makes Crichton novels so much fun, and the love interest seemed like an afterthought ("Oops, I forgot; we need to throw in a romance, or the book won't sell. Let's see...who's left?"). Considering the book was published after Crichton's death, I'm thinking they should have left well enough alone. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6051736906024296913" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1238458992" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1238458992" />
    <a title="Jesse A" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21013603-jesse-a"><img alt="Jesse A" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529526929p2/21013603.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<div id="review_401570714" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/401570714" />
    <a title="Wambeeno" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4341622-wambeeno"><img alt="Wambeeno" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/401570714?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wambeeno" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wambeeno" href="/user/show/4341622-wambeeno">Wambeeno</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer401570714&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer401570714" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12822652643155157494"><br/><br/>Eight Things I Learned from Reading Micro:<br/><br/>1. Hawaii must be a wonderland of plant biodiversity, because you can be half an inch tall and still find all of the ingredients for curare in the course of a single morning, not to mention the nut necessary to start a fire so you can cook said curare. <br/>2. The brutality of your death is inversely proportional to how interesting you are as a person. For instance, if you are a brilliant venom expert with strong leadership skills and great personal charisma,</span>
  <span id="freeText12822652643155157494" style="display:none"><br /><br />Eight Things I Learned from Reading Micro:<br /><br />1. Hawaii must be a wonderland of plant biodiversity, because you can be half an inch tall and still find all of the ingredients for curare in the course of a single morning, not to mention the nut necessary to start a fire so you can cook said curare. <br />2. The brutality of your death is inversely proportional to how interesting you are as a person. For instance, if you are a brilliant venom expert with strong leadership skills and great personal charisma, your death will be relatively quick and painless, whereas if you are a generic pharmaceutical company employee whose only purpose in life is to share important plot points and get eaten by ants, your death scene will take up half a page and include multiple references to your "viscera." <br />3. If you are a good-looking female martial arts expert and you're forced to utilize your skills in combat against giant insects, shouting "Yaah!" as you leap at your foe can really work some wonder.<br />4. If your friend has been envenomated by a wasp and has only minutes before his breathing shuts down, fear not--your superhuman arachnologist skills will allow you to locate the very spider whose venom is needed to concoct an antivenom; milk said venom from said spider; brew up your antivenom; and administer it to your friend, saving his life just in the nick of time. <br />5. If you are an insufferable little snot with no reason to live except to whine and endanger the lives of everyone on the team with your buffoonery, don't worry--you'll outlive almost everyone else because they are all apparently Mother Teresa reincarnated and would never leave you behind, not even to save themselves, whereas most normal people would have pushed you out of the airplane during the flight to Hawaii.<br />6. If you decide to flaunt your free-thinking and liberal nature by going for a skinny-dip and enticing your morally-minded friends to follow you like the Jezebel you are, you can bet you're not going to live for much longer. <br />7. It is apparently possible to write prose like the following and still get published: <blockquote>[He] tapped on the hatch cover with the tip of his knife, but that didn't work. The hatch was tightly shut and not even the tip of his knife would get it open.</blockquote> "Uh, Richard, I really like this scene with the hatch, I do. But I just feel it could use a little more tension." "I know! What if I make more references to the knife tip and how it can't open the hatch?" "Perfect!"<br />8. You can make boatloads of money--and bring shame to one of the most beloved authors of our time to boot--by writing tripe like something from one of those B-movies from the 70s they always used to show on TBS and then publishing it under some other guy's name. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12822652643155157494" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_263878927" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/263878927" />
    <a title="Kenny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541343-kenny"><img alt="Kenny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446678573p2/541343.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/263878927?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kenny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kenny" href="/user/show/541343-kenny">Kenny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/541343-kenny?shelf=fiction-scifi">fiction-scifi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer263878927" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18395870662659461289">Michael Crichton was an idea guy and apparently a busy man. So far, two books have been published with his name on them after his death. The first, <i>Pirate Latitudes</i>, was completed by Crichton, though the writing is uneven and thus it was probably edited after his death.<br/><br/><i>Micro</i> is another story. Clearly, the writing style in the first third of the book is Crichton's, with emphasis on the "gee whiz" scientific aspects of the story that so often (and welcomely) permeates his books. The last two third</span>
  <span id="freeText18395870662659461289" style="display:none">Michael Crichton was an idea guy and apparently a busy man. So far, two books have been published with his name on them after his death. The first, <i>Pirate Latitudes</i>, was completed by Crichton, though the writing is uneven and thus it was probably edited after his death.<br /><br /><i>Micro</i> is another story. Clearly, the writing style in the first third of the book is Crichton's, with emphasis on the "gee whiz" scientific aspects of the story that so often (and welcomely) permeates his books. The last two thirds, a pure adventure story, is clearly Richard Preston's, and departs from Crichton's usual precise but affecting style.<br /><br />While this is not necessarily a bad thing, the book leaves one flat, primarily because there are so many protagonists (7) that we're supposed to like and relate to, but one by one, they are killed off until the least likely of them survives. It's hard to believe Crichton would not have allowed the brother of an early (apparent) casualty in the book to survive, but he dies unceremoniously, and this is disconcerting. Crichton knew better, I think, than to do this. <br /><br />Preston, whose main experience is in dramatizing non-fiction, can get away with this sort of thing in his books because his stories usually have the added fantastical and sometimes nonsensical elements of factuality that readers often happily exchange for drama. After all, truth <i>is</i> stranger than fiction.<br /><br />That being said, I wish Crichton himself had finished this novel because his dramatic instincts are apparently better than Preston's. Clearly, this book began as a way to update two movie classics: <i>Fantastic Voyage,</i> and <i>The Incredible Shrinking Man</i>, past due, now that CGI capabilities make the apparent miniaturization of humans easy. <br /><br />But Crichton would have, I hope, in subsequent passes to his manuscript, edited the villian into a more believable person and given the quest of the little people a bit more tension and rising action. As it is, the antagonist is the most cardboard villian since Snidely Whiplash, and the events of the story seem scattershot, with no increasing tension beyond the de rigeur ticking clock, employed long ago to great effect in <i>Fantastic Voyage.</i><br /><br />I don't think this book will stand alongside Crichton classics such as <i>Jurassic Park</i> and <i>Andromeda Strain</i>, but it was an acceptable, quick read, a literary cotton candy: sugary, but not much there.<br /><br />We miss you, Mr. Crichton. RIP.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18395870662659461289" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_242235002" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/242235002" />
    <a title="Tiff Miller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4506462-tiff-miller"><img alt="Tiff Miller" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483923669p2/4506462.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/242235002?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tiff Miller" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tiff Miller" href="/user/show/4506462-tiff-miller">Tiff Miller</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4506462-tiff-miller?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4506462-tiff-miller?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4506462-tiff-miller?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer242235002" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13237658906808494381">**There are some "kind-of" spoilers, but if you've read any Crichton, you won't be surprised. Just FYI**<br/><br/>I opened this book yesterday, and finished it before I went to bed. I couldn't put it down. The concept was intriguing, and the glimpse into the micro-world was pretty cool. Like all Crichton books, it's graphic and descriptive in its violence. It has some language, for sure. And there's even a giant boob. The dialogue was often cheesy and predictable, but some of it was great. Of course, near</span>
  <span id="freeText13237658906808494381" style="display:none">**There are some "kind-of" spoilers, but if you've read any Crichton, you won't be surprised. Just FYI**<br /><br />I opened this book yesterday, and finished it before I went to bed. I couldn't put it down. The concept was intriguing, and the glimpse into the micro-world was pretty cool. Like all Crichton books, it's graphic and descriptive in its violence. It has some language, for sure. And there's even a giant boob. The dialogue was often cheesy and predictable, but some of it was great. Of course, nearly everyone dies in graphic, terrible ways, as is usual with Crichton's plot lines.<br /><br />It was begun by Crichton , but finished for him posthumously by Richard Preston. I have no idea how to compare their styles, and I don't know if Preston's vision changed Crichton's intent at all. So, no commentary on that.<br /><br />One of the problems I have with Crichton novels is their tendency to have a reallyfastbeginning and a reallyfastending. It's like he can't wait to get to the meat of the plot, so he takes very little time to fully establish characters. He still does a decent job in a few words, but it takes quite awhile for me to care about any of them. (There are some interesting twists in who Crichton kills off in this one, though, which was pretty nice. My accuracy in calling the deaths failed about half the time in this book! And I actually cared about a few of them...) Also, there's a transition scene that I found myself thinking "Where the heck did THAT come from so suddenly? Jeez..." I had to re-read it to make sure I hadn't accidentally skipped a page or two.<br /><br />He takes his time in the middle, building up some great tension on fantastic detail, then it's like he realizes, "Hey - I'm running out of pages. Better wind this up quick." And boy, does he! Still, the ending to this book was far more satisfying than the ending of "Sphere" or "Congo". (Frankly, those two books sucked, in my opinion. Could have been amazing, but weren't.)<br /><br />Ever since I read and loved "Jurassic Park," I tend to pick up his novels with anticipation, even though I've been disappointed more often than I enjoyed myself. I loved "Jurassic Park" a LOT, and still can't stop grabbing a Crichton novel when I see them. I think I love his attention to scientific detail, the depth of research obvious in his books, and the craziness of the adventures that seem somehow believable.<br /><br />This was classic Crichton, much more in line with "The Lost World" than any other of his books, I think. It was good, but not GREAT. I still really liked it, however. Hence, the 4-stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13237658906808494381" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_206739675" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/206739675" />
    <a title="Mith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3620424-mith"><img alt="Mith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1389091865p2/3620424.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/206739675?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mith" href="/user/show/3620424-mith">Mith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3620424-mith?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3620424-mith?shelf=goodish">goodish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3620424-mith?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3620424-mith?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer206739675" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12642965498787415516"><b>3.5 stars out of 5.</b><br/><br/>This book can be summed up in one gif -<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380358574ra/709753.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><b>Disclaimer:</b> A little suspension on disbelief is required by the reader to read Micro (or any other MC book, for that matter). <br/><br/><i>Nanigen</i> is a robotics company which recruits seven graduate students (each from a different field) as part of its research team. Nanigen is light-years ahead in its technology, seeing as how its scientists have come up with a "tensor generator" which is able to shrink humans to micro sizes. Teams have been successf</span>
  <span id="freeText12642965498787415516" style="display:none"><b>3.5 stars out of 5.</b><br><br>This book can be summed up in one gif -<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380358574ra/709753.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Disclaimer:</b> A little suspension on disbelief is required by the reader to read Micro (or any other MC book, for that matter). <br><br><i>Nanigen</i> is a robotics company which recruits seven graduate students (each from a different field) as part of its research team. Nanigen is light-years ahead in its technology, seeing as how its scientists have come up with a "tensor generator" which is able to shrink humans to micro sizes. Teams have been successfully shrunk and sent into the forests to study micro-organisms, most of which have never even been identified by mankind till now.<br><br>As it turns out, the CEO of Nanigen is a bad, bad man. Because of a scandal that one of the graduates uncovers, the CEO, in his rage, shrinks the graduates and abandons them in the forest teeming with predetors of <i>all</i> sizes. The seven of them have to survive against all odds and return back to normal size somehow before the "micro-bends" (a suddenly manifesting disease that causes internal hemorrhaging which eventually leads to death) gets them.<br><br>A classic Crichton book. I can honestly say I'll never be able to look at the world around me the same way again. We never usually pay attention to all the tiny creatures <i>in</i> and around us - <i>the millions of bacteria inside us, the pretty butterflies flitting around, the centipedes, millipedes and ants crawling everywhere, the strange unnamed worms or mites we (I) carefully step around</i> - in our day-to-day life but Crichton brings them to attention in a horrifyingly gripping way (I had to put down the book many times because I was too grossed out to continue!). Let me just tell you now - The ants that you see scurrying around minding their own business are NOTHING like the cute and friendly ants you saw in "Honey, I shrunk the kids", when you are their size.<br><br>*shudder*<br><br>But it's not all bad, too. Crichton also offers us a glimpse into how the world works from the very heart of it. There is one scene in particular where one of the characters plays with a paramecium - that's a protozoan, usually invisible to the naked eye; I thought that was pretty cool :)<br><br>Unfortunately, in the end, I was able to give this book only 3.5 stars. For me, the major let down was <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the death of, who was till then, the <i>main character</i>. After that the focus shifts to the secondary characters and, while it is definitely unorthodox, it also felt very clumsy to me - maybe it could have been handled better under a different author. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Also, all the deaths in book felt forced. It's like the characters all died just for the sake of dying - none of the deaths had any purpose. <br><br>The ending, as well, fell a bit flat but unlike to the destination, the journey was amazing :) Recommended.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="12642965498787415516" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_626820146" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/626820146" />
    <a title="Kristine Muslim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/808098-kristine-muslim"><img alt="Kristine Muslim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596960436p2/808098.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/626820146?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristine Muslim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristine Muslim" href="/user/show/808098-kristine-muslim">Kristine Muslim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007350001" href="/book/show/13604348-micro">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer626820146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2033876351720456740">I read this in one sitting today. Then I realized that there would be no more new Michael Crichton books after I finish his other posthumous novel, and what a sad finality to have to finally read what Crichton was reportedly working on when he was battling cancer. Now I'll simply have to make do with rereading his previous books. The choice of Richard Preston, the guy who wrote the harrowing <i>The Hot Zone</i> and oh-yes-yes <i>Cobra Event</i> and whose pedigree includes being a brother to don't-get-me-start</span>
  <span id="freeText2033876351720456740" style="display:none">I read this in one sitting today. Then I realized that there would be no more new Michael Crichton books after I finish his other posthumous novel, and what a sad finality to have to finally read what Crichton was reportedly working on when he was battling cancer. Now I'll simply have to make do with rereading his previous books. The choice of Richard Preston, the guy who wrote the harrowing <i>The Hot Zone</i> and oh-yes-yes <i>Cobra Event</i> and whose pedigree includes being a brother to don't-get-me-started Douglas Preston of Preston/Child fame, to finish Crichton's book is perfect. <i>Micro</i> is classic Crichton, the quintessential man vs. natural world theme, the always man "vs." nature and not "with," a distinctive statement by Crichton, strong intelligent women characters, and lots and lots of infodump, which is always a good thing no matter what critics and writing teachers say. And if you are into infodumps, there's the mother lode, Arthur Herzog's <i>Heat</i>, dry as the paper it's written on but still manages to be charming. After reading <i>Micro</i>, I've learned numerous life-saving biochem techniques (viable or not) should someone shrink me to around half an inch in size while I'm having a great time in the middle of a forested area in a volcanic island like Hawaii. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2033876351720456740" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_292364096" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/292364096" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/292364096?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780007350032" href="/book/show/13068632-micro">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer292364096" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14420342634319443921">My first and so far last Michael Crichton Novel was "Jurassic Park". Now I've read "Miro". Again, the author(s) explore(s) a part of new technology and the dark sides of it. But as expected from Crichton he did it in a highly realistic and intelligent way. The book definitely is a page-turner with a lot of surprises! I especially liked the descriptions of the Hawaiian fauna and flora, the green hell the students suddently found themselves in. A lot of fun, definitely to be recommended.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_3518209190" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3518209190" />
    <a title="Erin Clemence" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16907451-erin-clemence"><img alt="Erin Clemence" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461798513p2/16907451.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3518209190?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erin Clemence" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erin Clemence" href="/user/show/16907451-erin-clemence">Erin Clemence</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060873172" href="/book/show/13623736-micro">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3518209190" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4540829093959817619">“Micro” comes from the brilliant mind of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40604658.Jurassic_Park__Jurassic_Park___1_' title='Jurassic Park (Jurassic Park, #1) by Michael Crichton' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jurassic Park</a> writer, Michael Crichton. This novel was the last one Mr. Crichton worked on before his untimely death, and in fact, he passed before it was finished, which is where Richard Preston steps in. <br/><br/><i> A group of young scientists, working in the finest scientific laboratories in Boston, are invited to Hawaii as part of a recruitment project by start-up entrepreneur Vincent Drake. Here, the group is asked to be part of next-level scientific discovery </i></span>
  <span id="freeText4540829093959817619" style="display:none">“Micro” comes from the brilliant mind of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40604658.Jurassic_Park__Jurassic_Park___1_" title="Jurassic Park (Jurassic Park, #1) by Michael Crichton" rel="nofollow noopener">Jurassic Park</a> writer, Michael Crichton. This novel was the last one Mr. Crichton worked on before his untimely death, and in fact, he passed before it was finished, which is where Richard Preston steps in. <br /><br /><i> A group of young scientists, working in the finest scientific laboratories in Boston, are invited to Hawaii as part of a recruitment project by start-up entrepreneur Vincent Drake. Here, the group is asked to be part of next-level scientific discovery project hemmed by Drake, as he seeks to develop micro robots that would be able to explore parts of the Hawaiian biosphere that have as of yet been unable to be explored by humans. Jumping at the chance, the group are soon learning about Drake’s powerful technologies and advanced scientific machines. However, the group soon realizes that Drake has a far more sinister plan, manufacturing miniature aircraft and weaponry, and that Drake will go to extreme levels to keep his discoveries a secret. </i> <br /><br />Crichton has a way of writing scientific, intelligent novels that are both interesting and completely readable. There is no heavy scientific text or concepts, and Crichton is able to bring the important scientific facts to a readers’ level, as he did with “<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40604658.Jurassic_Park__Jurassic_Park___1_" title="Jurassic Park (Jurassic Park, #1) by Michael Crichton" rel="nofollow noopener">Jurassic Park</a>” <i> (another one of Crichton’s novels that I thoroughly enjoyed). </i><br /><br />Right from the onset, this novel pulls you in. Maybe I’m showing my age, but I was a bit nostalgic for “Honey, I Shrunk the Kids!” <i> (props to Canadian Rick Moranis) </i> during Crichton’s “little people, big world” scenes. That being said, the novel was not at all silly as you might assume, and was every bit as entertaining as you would expect from a scientific novel of Crichton’s. <br /><br />The characters were honest and exceptionally brilliant, and the story was well-told and well-researched. I <i> think </i> I was able to tell where Mr. Preston took over, as I noticed a small difference in the storytelling <i> (but not enough to make any difference to the level of enjoyment) </i>. <br /><br />I have just started to read Crichton <i> (although obviously, I’ve been familiar with his work for many years) </i>, and every novel I read continues to surprise me with its realism and the ability to engage a reader so completely. Although it isn’t a light read by any means, “Micro” is a highly entertaining one! <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4540829093959817619" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_307353735" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/307353735" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/729380-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442456477p2/729380.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/307353735?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/729380-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer307353735" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17590865571112455821">I have no problem proclaiming my love and adoration of the late, great Michael Crichton. Where he has always lacked in characterization and crafting language, he has always told big stories with big ideas. He was first and foremost an ideas man, and much like Jules Verne 150 years earlier, the concepts took center stage while characters and whatnot hung back in the shadows, only peeking out when necessary to the plot.<br/><br/>This is probably my least favorite Crichton novel, and I've read them all (excl</span>
  <span id="freeText17590865571112455821" style="display:none">I have no problem proclaiming my love and adoration of the late, great Michael Crichton. Where he has always lacked in characterization and crafting language, he has always told big stories with big ideas. He was first and foremost an ideas man, and much like Jules Verne 150 years earlier, the concepts took center stage while characters and whatnot hung back in the shadows, only peeking out when necessary to the plot.<br /><br />This is probably my least favorite Crichton novel, and I've read them all (excluding Pirate Latitudes, his other posthuumous release, which I will read next).<br /><br />For the first 100 pages, I had considerable trouble even remembering who was who in the book. There were seven or eight grad students who end up in the micro-world of Hawaii, and all of them were virtually interchangeable (except for Rick Hutter, whose spiteful attitude anyone could remember). I was also put off by the over-the-top cartoon villain quality of the novel's protagonist Vin Drake. His motivations and modus operandi are among the stupidest things I have recently encountered in a novel. I found his character ridiculous, and (SPOILER) his eventual demise left me quite dissatisfied and wanting more. This man's crimes were poorly planned, ludicrous, and illogical. How the hell did he even get the kids into the magnet room in the first place? Absurd.<br /><br />There are also some pretty big lapses in logic. I don't need to get into all of them, but they are apparent when you're reading. Further, when someone dies (as they are apt to do in this frightening landscape), no feelings really come out of it, because I wasn't sure who was dying at times, and others I just didn't care. In Crichton's other books this is also a problem, but never more so than here. In fact, in his other novels, you could feel a palpable terror when reading some passages. I found the squid scenes in "Sphere" terrifying, the dangers of the Congo in, well, "Congo" to be spine-tingling, and the dinosaur attacks in the "Park" books riveting. Here, I found a lot of the narration just annoying, and instead of sitting on the edge of my seat, I was fighting the urge to stop reading such a poorly written narrative.<br /><br />The writing is lazy here. Too many adverbs in the quote tags, too many unnecessary uses of "and" in the lists, redundant vocabulary, poor syntax. This is the English teacher in me speaking, but I am sure any intelligent reader will take notice. Some of the work here is reminiscent of old classmates I had back in college, whose work sucked out loud, and who never deserved to be published. And never were.<br /><br />So why the two stars, and not one? Well...I love Michael Crichton, and have always considered him a fascinating writer who loved spinning a good high-adventure yarn. Despite the fact Preston finished this one, I still don't want to bash the dead too much. The other reason is, I would only give a one-star reviiew to a book I didn't finish. I finished this, because I just had to see where it was going. I saw that finish line...and I was disappointed, through and through.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17590865571112455821" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_269415798" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/269415798" />
    <a title="Kerry Nietz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2945523-kerry-nietz"><img alt="Kerry Nietz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472305007p2/2945523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/269415798?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kerry Nietz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kerry Nietz" href="/user/show/2945523-kerry-nietz">Kerry Nietz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer269415798" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17184552104524909082">I have to admit, I was concerned. The reviews of “Micro” seemed so lackluster that I was almost afraid to read it. I wondered if it would taint the memory of an author I really admire. Sure, I knew the reigns were handed off to Richard Preston—a notable author in his own right—but as an author myself, I also know how difficult it would be to pick up someone else’s work and not only finish it, but do it in a way that would please that author’s fans. An author’s voice is unique. To duplicate it wo</span>
  <span id="freeText17184552104524909082" style="display:none">I have to admit, I was concerned. The reviews of “Micro” seemed so lackluster that I was almost afraid to read it. I wondered if it would taint the memory of an author I really admire. Sure, I knew the reigns were handed off to Richard Preston—a notable author in his own right—but as an author myself, I also know how difficult it would be to pick up someone else’s work and not only finish it, but do it in a way that would please that author’s fans. An author’s voice is unique. To duplicate it would be like trying to duplicate someone’s handwriting. So how well did Preston do?<br /><br />I think he did a fantastic job. “Micro” is a page-turner in every sense of the word. It has lots of fascinating cutting-edge science, plenty of action and intrigue, surprises, a villain you can despise, a scientific team working to solve problems—in short, all the things you expect from a Michael Crichton book. And yet, it was unique enough to stand on its own. To me, it felt like “Jurassic Park” on a microscopic scale. I mean that as a complement, of course, as “Park” was one of my favorite Crichton books.<br /><br />My only criticism would come early in the book. One of the characters reacts to a death in the family in a way that felt, to me, a little unauthentic. I can’t imagine a real person acting the way he/she acted in similar circumstances. However, I also realize that his/her responses were part of the setup for the amazing stuff that comes later. And I can overlook a lot for what comes later. It is awesome.<br /><br />“Micro” is a worthy addition to the Crichton canon. Well done, Mr. Preston!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17184552104524909082" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3386060181" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3386060181" />
    <a title="Twerking To Beethoven" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8940772-twerking-to-beethoven"><img alt="Twerking To Beethoven" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559489595p2/8940772.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3386060181?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Twerking To Beethoven" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Twerking To Beethoven" href="/user/show/8940772-twerking-to-beethoven">Twerking To Beethoven</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3386060181" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3788088432772653764">What would you get if you mixed "Fantastic Voyage", "Honey, I Shrunk the Kids" and "The Island of Doctor Moreau"? This book, that's what you'd get. "Micro" is a super-fun novel. Nothing spectacular, all right, but some bits were so over the top they made the whole book awesome. I'm well aware of the fact that it wasn't met with critical acclaim, still it's a five star read for me. <br/><br/>PS = Given the amount of scientific-technical content, I'm pretty sure the first half of the book is 24 carat Cricht</span>
  <span id="freeText3788088432772653764" style="display:none">What would you get if you mixed "Fantastic Voyage", "Honey, I Shrunk the Kids" and "The Island of Doctor Moreau"? This book, that's what you'd get. "Micro" is a super-fun novel. Nothing spectacular, all right, but some bits were so over the top they made the whole book awesome. I'm well aware of the fact that it wasn't met with critical acclaim, still it's a five star read for me. <br /><br />PS = Given the amount of scientific-technical content, I'm pretty sure the first half of the book is 24 carat Crichton, while the second half is mainly Richard Preston.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3788088432772653764" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1338659184" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1338659184" />
    <a title="Rade " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8187431-rade"><img alt="Rade " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1426096920p2/8187431.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1338659184?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rade " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rade " href="/user/show/8187431-rade">Rade </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060873172" href="/book/show/13623736-micro">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8187431-rade?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1338659184" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17941680611944039349">OK, before I start with my review i just want to point out that a movie based on this book is rumored to be in the works. I believe I read Disney bought the rights for it and the other Crichton's book called <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6428887.Pirate_Latitudes' title='Pirate Latitudes by Michael Crichton' rel='nofollow noopener'>Pirate Latitudes</a>. This could be good and bad since it is sure to be rated PG or PG-13 with minimum on screen violence. Basically, it could be just another version of <i>Honey, I shrunk the Kids</i> movie. <br/><br/>Anyway, on to the book. The concept of small humans surviving the environment and being chased</span>
  <span id="freeText17941680611944039349" style="display:none">OK, before I start with my review i just want to point out that a movie based on this book is rumored to be in the works. I believe I read Disney bought the rights for it and the other Crichton's book called <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6428887.Pirate_Latitudes" title="Pirate Latitudes by Michael Crichton" rel="nofollow noopener">Pirate Latitudes</a>. This could be good and bad since it is sure to be rated PG or PG-13 with minimum on screen violence. Basically, it could be just another version of <i>Honey, I shrunk the Kids</i> movie. <br /><br />Anyway, on to the book. The concept of small humans surviving the environment and being chased by animals that we find miniscule is by no means a new concept. It has been done plenty of times in the past. This time it is a bit different since it got grad students who are experts in different field concerning bug and plant world being thrown into a world that is filled with bugs and plants, making their survival somewhat plausible. The problem is that all the characters, and I do mean ALL OF THEM, are one dimensional and boring as all hell. Not going to list them, but trust me when I say it, I hated all of them. Some are stupid, some are assholes, some are self-centered, and others act tough for the sake of acting tough. I hated Danny. He was the worst of the bunch. There was one character I liked but he got killed. <br /><br />I really liked the concept of this book. You got these humans encountering small insects who are either harmless or extremely dangerous and fighting their way to survive. Of course, some of the scenes were ridiculous and relied way too much on the incredible luck and big coincidences but still a fun read. I really enjoyed how the humans fought against these insects, teaming together to find food, weapons, and reasons to keep on going. The small facts here and there about the insects kept me going. I just found it interesting how some creepy crawlers with numerous legs and appearance of incredible killing machines tend to be rather harmless to humans because they eat plants. <br /><br />You can probably guess what will happen in the end but even though it is predictable, it works. The "bad guy" reminded me of one of those James Bong villains, except he did not sit in a giant chair stroking a cat while explaining his plan to the heroes. He was as stereotypical as they get though.<br /><br />Overall an enjoyable book but I can definitely see why some people hated it. I don't understand all the one star reviews but than again I've given popular books one star ratings while others praised the shit out of them. I just hope they don't mess up the movie based on this book, but I feel like it will be much better if it was a mini series on TV channels such as AMC where it can be shown in all its bloody gory. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17941680611944039349" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/241530681?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tanja Berg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tanja Berg" href="/user/show/5935491-tanja-berg">Tanja Berg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer241530681" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15170102682376073167">This is not a good book. It's even worse than Michael Crichton's "Next". Despite this, I actually quite enjoyed it, probably because it was a quick and entertaining read. No surprises or twists and a very easy plot. The experience can be compared to eating a big mac and drinking a cheap wine with it - it fills you up for the moment while numbing your brain.<br/><br/>It's very difficult to give any sort of synopsis without revealing too much. I had feared the book would be very much like "Prey", but it's n</span>
  <span id="freeText15170102682376073167" style="display:none">This is not a good book. It's even worse than Michael Crichton's "Next". Despite this, I actually quite enjoyed it, probably because it was a quick and entertaining read. No surprises or twists and a very easy plot. The experience can be compared to eating a big mac and drinking a cheap wine with it - it fills you up for the moment while numbing your brain.<br /><br />It's very difficult to give any sort of synopsis without revealing too much. I had feared the book would be very much like "Prey", but it's not. The tiny little robots in this case are differentely equipped. The setting is the jungle in Hawaii, with eight students lost in it with their varying competenices. It's a bit like "Ten Little Indians" - one by one they die horrible unexpected deaths. This is not a book for the squeamish and particularly not for arachnophobics. Arachnophobia aside, there is a scene involving a centipede that truly reminds me of how much I hate these creatures. They evoke a "kill kill kill" response in me that many seem to have for spiders. I don't mind spiders and I never kill them intentionally. Centipedes though, are seriously disgusting creatures with too many segments and way too many legs. They also move at alarming speed and not always away from you.<br /><br />And yes, that is what I am left with. A disgusting scene with a centipede. It's ten minutes since I finished the book and it is already fading from memory.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15170102682376073167" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
